Distinguishing Divine Love and Will in the Quran
Maturidi explains the difference between love/pleasure and will/volition using Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic basis for distinguishing between God's love/pleasure and His will/volition, and why this distinction is important for understanding divine attributes and human responsibility.

Core Idea
God's love is specific to good; His will is universal.

Maturidi opens this passage by grounding a critical theological distinction in the Quran itself. He argues that while God's will (mashi'ah) encompasses all things, His love (mahabbah) and pleasure (rida) are exclusively directed towards what is good. This distinction resolves apparent contradictions in the Quran and avoids the error of attributing evil to God's love.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic evidence
The passage begins with four Quranic verses. Identify each verse and notice how they are used to differentiate between love/pleasure and will.
Pay attention to the verbs: 'loves', 'is pleased', 'wills'.
2
Understand the principle
Maturidi explains that love and displeasure are 'caused by the actions of servants' while will is not. Reflect on why this matters for divine justice.
This is the core theoretical move.
3
Examine the human analogy
The passage draws a parallel to human experience: a person may do what he dislikes but not what he does not will. Consider how this supports the distinction.
Think about the difference between coercive will and reactive love.
4
Note the theological stance
Maturidi contrasts his view (will accompanies action) with another view (will precedes action). Recognize the significance of this difference for qadar.
This shapes how we understand God's relation to human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين المحبة والرضا، وبين الإرادة والمشيئة بقوله: وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكُفْرَ، وقوله: وَالله لَا يُحِبُّ الفَسَادَ، وقوله: إِنَّ اللهَ يُحِبُّ التَّوَّابِينَ، لا يُحِبُّ المُعْتَدِينَ، وقال في المشيئة: مَنْ يَشَإِ اللهُ يُضْلِلْهُ وَمَنْ يَشَأْ يَجْعَلْهُ عَلَى صِرَاطٍ مُسْتَقِيمٍ، وغير ذلك مما يوجب تخصيص المحبة والرضا وتعميم المشيئة والإرادة، يوصف بهما في أفعاله، ولا يوصف بالرضا والمحبة، على أن المشيئة صرفها إلى القوة حتى جعلها بحكم القسر، فلذلك قوتها توجب ذلك. والأصل في ذلك أن المحبة والسخط معنيان يوجبان بفعل العباد، وليست المشيئة كذلك؛ لما ليس في أفعال العباد معنى يوجب المشيئة، إلا أن يراد بها الرضا أو التمني، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وفي الشاهد قد يفعل الرجل ما لا يرضى به ولا يحبه، ومحال حقيقة فعل لا يريده، وكذا معنى الإرادة متقدم عندهم على الفعل، وعندنا معنى يكون معه، ولا وجه لها بعده، وأمر الرضا والسخط والمحبة ونحو ذلك يكون من بعد في المتعارف أبدا، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The difference between love and pleasure, and between will and volition, by His saying: 'And He is not pleased with disbelief for His servants,' and His saying: 'Allah does not love corruption,' and His saying: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent, and does not love the transgressors,' and He said concerning volition: 'Whomever Allah wills He misguides, and whomever He wills He places upon a straight path,' and other than that which necessitates the specification of love and pleasure and the generalization of volition and will. He is described by them in His acts, and is not described by pleasure and love, because volition is diverted to power until it makes it by the rule of compulsion; therefore its power necessitates that. The principle in that is that love and displeasure are two meanings caused by the actions of servants, and will is not like that, because there is not in the actions of servants a meaning that necessitates will, unless by it is meant pleasure or wish. And there is no power except with Allah. And in the witnessed, a man may do what he is not pleased with nor loves, but it is impossible in reality to do what he does not will. Similarly, the meaning of will, according to them, precedes the action, and according to us, it is a meaning that accompanies it, and there is no aspect for it after. And the matter of pleasure, displeasure, love, and the like are always after in the conventional. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 896

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the distinction between love/pleasure (محبة/رضا) and will/volition (إرادة/مشيئة) based on Quranic verses. Maturidi cites four verses: that God is not pleased with disbelief, does not love corruption, loves the repentant but not transgressors, and regarding volition that He guides and misguides. These show that love and pleasure are specified to good, while will and volition are general. He then explains that volition is directed to power and compulsion, so its power necessitates that. The underlying principle is that love and displeasure are caused by the actions of servants, but will is not—it is not caused by the servants' actions. In human experience, a person may do what he does not love or like, but he cannot do what he does not will. Finally, he notes that for some, will precedes action, but for Maturidi, will accompanies the action, while love and pleasure come after.

Key Terms
محبة
رضا
إرادة
مشيئة
كراهة
سخط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will and love are identical; He loves everything that happens.
Correction: The Quran distinguishes them: God wills all things but loves only good; He may will evil without loving it.

Checkpoint
What is the origin of love and displeasure according to Maturidi in this passage?
A) They are caused by God's will
B) They are caused by the actions of servants
C) They are eternal attributes
D) They are unrelated to actions

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that God's love and pleasure are specific to good, while His will and volition are general, reconciling divine justice with providence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do something you know God loves (like repentance) but your own will resists, do you recognize that your will is not the same as God's love? How does this distinction challenge your excuses for sin?
?
If God wills everything that happens, including your sins, yet He does not love them, how does that change your understanding of His mercy and your responsibility?
?
In what area of your life have you assumed God's will and His love are the same, leading you to either justify sin or despair of His mercy? What will you change now?

The Objection of the Verse 'Allah Wills Ease for You' and Its Answer
Maturidi answers the objection using the verse about God willing ease and not hardship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi responds to the objection that the verse 'Allah wills ease for you' proves God wills faith for everyone, by explaining that 'will' here means permission and concession, and that the verse addresses believers.

Core Idea
God's will in the 'ease' verse means permission, not decree of faith.

In this passage, Maturidi tackles a potential misinterpretation of Quran 2:185. Critics might argue that since God wills ease and not hardship, and disbelief is the hardest hardship, God must not will disbelief. Maturidi offers a nuanced answer that clarifies the meaning of 'will' in this context. Let's read his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection presented and the two-part answer that follows.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that since God does not will hardship (and disbelief is the hardest hardship), He must will faith for everyone. Find this in the text.
3
Study the first part of the answer
Maturidi responds that 'will' in this verse means permission and license, not the kind of will that determines belief. This defuses the objection.
This is a key distinction in kalam.
4
Study the second part of the answer
Even if the verse referred to both ease and hardship, it would apply to believers, not disbelievers. The final sentence implies that if God willed faith for a disbeliever, he would believe.
This reinforces the point that God does not will faith for everyone.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this interpretation protects the doctrine of divine will without attributing injustice to God. The verse is about concession, not about universal faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بقوله تعالى: يُرِيدُ اللهُ بِكُمُ اليُسْرَ، ونحو ذلك، وقال: وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ العُسْرَ، والكفر أعسر العسر. قيل: الإرادة في هذا تخرج على الإذن والإباحة والرخصة، ليس ذلك من أمر الإيمان في شيء، فكذا إرادة العسر. وأيضا أنه لو كان على الأمرين فالوجه أن أولئك قوم قد آمنوا فلم يكن لهم في التحقيق غير الذي أراد، فلو كان من الكافر أراد الإيمان لكان.
Then he argued with His saying, the Exalted: 'Allah wills ease for you,' and the like, and He said: 'And He does not will hardship for you,' and disbelief is the hardest of hardship. It was said: 'Will in this is interpreted as permission, allowance, and concession; that is not from the matter of faith at all, so likewise the willing of hardship.' And also that if it were upon both matters, the aspect is that those people had believed, so in reality they had nothing other than what He willed. So if He willed faith from the disbeliever, then it would be (he would be a believer).
Source pages p. 896

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding the verse that Allah wills ease for you (Quran 2:185) and does not will hardship. The objector argues that since disbelief is the hardest hardship, God's not willing hardship implies He does not will disbelief. The answer is that God's will in this context refers to permission, allowance, and license (الإذن والإباحة والرخصة), not to faith itself. Therefore, it does not show that God wills faith for everyone. Additionally, if the verse were about both the ease of faith and hardship of disbelief, then it refers to those who have already believed; for the disbeliever, if God had willed faith for him, he would believe. The implication is that God does not will faith for the disbeliever in the sense of decreeing it.

Key Terms
إرادة
إذن
إباحة
رخصة
يسر
عسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Allah wills ease for you' proves that God wills faith for every individual.
Correction: Maturidi explains that 'will' here means permission and concession, not divine decree. The verse addresses believers, not disbelievers.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi interpret the word 'will' (إرادة) in the verse 'Allah wills ease for you'?
A) As divine decree
B) As permission and concession
C) As love
D) As command

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's interpretation of 'will' as permission in the verse protects the doctrine that God does not will disbelief for anyone in a decree sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a verse supports your desired outcome without considering the precise meaning of its words? How does this lesson call you to be more careful with Allah's book?
?
This passage shows that God's 'will' can mean permission, not just decree. In what area of your life do you need to submit to God's decree rather than assume His permission? What would change if you accepted His will as decree?

Distinguishing Divine Love from Divine Will
Maturidi distinguishes love/pleasure from will/decree with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental distinction between God's love/pleasure and His will/decree according to Imam Maturidi, and how Quranic evidence supports this distinction.

Core Idea
God's will is universal, but His love is conditional upon moral goodness.

Imam Maturidi addresses a crucial question: Does God's will to create all things mean He loves and approves of everything? The following passage draws on the Quran to show that God may will something without loving it. This distinction clarifies divine justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic evidence
Maturidi cites verses showing God does not love corruption or disbelief, but He does love repentance. Notice the pattern.
2
Analyze the principle
He states that love and pleasure are contingent on the servant's action, while will is unconditional.
This is the key distinction.
3
Consider the analogy
In human experience, one can do what one does not love, but cannot truly act without willing it. This supports the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين المحبة والرضا، وبين الإرادة والمشيئة بقوله: (وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الْكُفْرَ) [الزمر:7]، وقوله: (وَالله لَا يُحِبُّ الْفَسَادَ) [البقرة:205]، وقوله: (إِنَّ اللهَ يُحِبُّ التَّوَّابِينَ) [البقرة:222]، (لَا يُحِبُّ الْمُعْتَدِينَ) [البقرة:190]، وقال في المشيئة: (مَنْ يَشَإِ اللَّهُ يُضْلِلْهُ وَمَنْ يَشَأْ يَجْعَلْهُ عَلَى صِرَاطٍ مُسْتَقِيمٍ) [الأنعام:125]، وغير ذلك مما يوجب تخصيص المحبة والرضا [و] تعميم المشيئة والإرادة، يوصف بهما في أفعاله، ولا يوصف بالرضا والمحبة، على أن المشيئة صرفها إلى القوة حتى جعلها بحكم القسر، فلذلك قوتها توجب ذلك. والأصل في ذلك أن المحبة والسخط معنيان يوجبان بفعل العباد، وليست المشيئة كذلك؛ لما ليس في أفعال العباد معنى يوجب المشيئة، إلا أن يراد بها الرضا أو التمنى، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وفي الشاهد قد يفعل الرجل ما لا يرضى به ولا يحبه، ومحال حقيقة فعل لا يريده، وكذا معنى الإرادة متقدم عندهم على الفعل، وعندنا معنى يكون معه، ولا وجه لها بعده، وأمر الرضا والسخط والمحبة ونحو ذلك يكون من بعد في المتعارف أبدا، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The distinction between love and pleasure, and between will and decree, by His statement: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' [39:7], and His statement: 'And Allah does not love corruption' [2:205], and His statement: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent' [2:222], 'He does not love the transgressors' [2:190], and He said concerning decree: 'Whomsoever Allah wills He leaves astray and whomsoever He wills He places upon a straight path' [6:125], and other such things that necessitate specifying love and pleasure and generalizing decree and will; He is described by them in His acts, but not described by pleasure and love, although decree is turned into compulsion until it is made by force, therefore its strength necessitates that. The foundation in this is that love and displeasure are meanings that are necessitated by the actions of servants, but decree is not so; because there is not in the actions of servants a meaning that necessitates decree, unless it is intended by it pleasure or wish, and there is no power except with Allah. And in witnessed reality, a man may do what he is not pleased with and does not love, and it is impossible in reality to have an action he does not will, and likewise the meaning of will is prior in their view to the action, and in our view it is a meaning that occurs with it, and there is no aspect for it after it, and the matter of pleasure, displeasure, love and the like is always afterwards in common usage, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 896

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument distinguishing between divine love/pleasure (المحبة والرضا) and divine will/decree (الإرادة والمشيئة). Maturidi begins by citing Quranic verses that show love and pleasure are specific to good actions (disbelief is not approved, corruption is not loved, but repentance is loved), while decree is general (God leads astray whom He wills). He then states the principle: love and displeasure are meanings contingent upon the action of the servant, whereas decree is not contingent; nothing in the servant's action necessitates decree. In observable experience, a person may do what he does not love or approve, but cannot truly act without willing it. Also, they say will precedes action, but Maturidi says will is simultaneous with the action. Love and pleasure always come after the action in common understanding. This establishes that God's will is universal and unconditional, whereas His love and pleasure are conditional upon the moral quality of the action.

Key Terms
المحبة
الرضا
الإرادة
المشيئة
السخط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will for something implies His approval of it.
Correction: Maturidi shows via Quran that God wills disbelief (as part of His decree) but does not approve of it (Quran 39:7).

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the fundamental difference between divine love/pleasure and divine will/decree?
A) Love and pleasure are eternal while will is temporal.
B) Love and pleasure are conditional on the servant's action, while will is unconditional and general.
C) Will is only for good things, while love is for all things.
D) There is no difference; they are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
God's will encompasses all things, but His love is restricted to what is morally good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience hardship or see evil in the world, do you assume that God's will implies His pleasure with that situation? How does this lesson challenge you to separate God's universal decree from His moral love? What would change if you truly believed that God may will what He does not love?
?
Think of an action you have done that you knew was wrong. Did you ever feel that because God allowed it, He must have approved? How does Maturidi's distinction between will and love call you to take full responsibility for your choices without using God's decree as an excuse?
?
If God's love is a response to the moral quality of your actions, ask yourself: what kind of actions are you performing that invite His love? Are you living in a way that assumes His pleasure or just His permission? What one change can you make today to seek His love rather than merely His will?

Interpreting Divine Will as Permission and Facilitation
Maturidi interprets God's will for ease as permission and facilitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that God's will for ease implies He wills faith for all, by distinguishing volitional will from permission.

Core Idea
When God wills ease, He permits and facilitates; He does not decree faith for everyone.

The opponent quotes verses where God says He intends ease for you and not hardship, arguing that since faith is ease and disbelief is hardship, God must will faith for all. Maturidi responds by explaining that 'will' here means permission and allowance, not volitional decree. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine will in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and verses
Read the Arabic passage containing the opponent's citation of verses 2:185 and Maturidi's initial response. Note the key terms.
2
Identify Maturidi's answer
Find where Maturidi says 'the will in this is interpreted as permission, allowance, and concession'. Understand that he is reinterpreting the meaning of will.
3
Understand the three terms
Reflect on the meanings of الإذن (permission), الإباحة (allowance), and الرخصة (concession). How do they differ from volitional will?
These terms indicate that God allows ease without necessarily decreeing faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بقوله تعالى: (يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ) [البقرة:185]، ونحو ذلك، وقال: (وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ) [البقرة:185]، والكفر أعسر العسر. قيل: الإرادة في هذا تخرج على الإذن والإباحة والرخصة، ليس ذلك من أمر الإيمان في شيء، فكذا إرادة العسر. وأيضًا أنه لو كان على الأمرين فالوجه [أن] أولئك قوم قد آمنوا فلم يكن لهم في التحقيق غير الذي أراد، فلو كان من الكافر أراد الإيمان لكان لا يكون سواه، كما إذا أراد فيمن أراد الإيمان لم يكن غيره، وعلى هذا قوله تعالى: (فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللَّهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ) [الأنعام:125]، وأيد ذلك قوله: (يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ أَلَّا يَجْعَلَ لَهُمْ حَقًّا فِي الْآخِرَةِ) [آل عمران:176]، وبين في المؤمنين: (وَاللَّهُ يُرِيدُ الْآخِرَةَ) [الأنفال:67]، دل أن كل من أريد له الإيمان أن يكون فعله أراد له الآخرة، ومن لم يرد لا، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he argued with His statement: 'Allah intends for you ease' [2:185], and similar, and He said: 'And does not intend for you hardship' [2:185], and disbelief is the most severe hardship. It was said: The will in this is interpreted as permission, allowance, and concession, that is not from the matter of faith in anything, so likewise the will of hardship. And also if it were upon both matters, the consideration is that those people had believed, so in reality there was nothing other than what He willed. For if He willed for the disbeliever faith, he would be nothing else, just as when He wills for one whom He wills faith, he is nothing else. And upon this is His statement: 'So whoever Allah wants to guide' [6:125], and that is supported by His statement: 'Allah intends that He give them no share in the Hereafter' [3:176], and He made clear concerning the believers: 'And Allah desires the Hereafter' [8:67]. This indicates that for everyone for whom faith is intended, his action is that He intended for him the Hereafter, and for whom He does not intend, not. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 896

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent cites verses where God says He wills ease for people, implying He wills faith (since faith is ease, disbelief is hardship). Maturidi gives the answer: the 'will' here means permission and facilitation (الإذن والإباحة والرخصة), not volitional decree. Disbelief is indeed the greatest hardship, but God does not will it in the sense of permission. Then he adds a second argument: if God's will were only for faith, then believers would have only faith and disbelievers would have faith, but that is not the case. He supports this with verses that show God wills guidance for some but not others, and that He does not will a share in the Hereafter for certain people, while He wills the Hereafter for believers. Thus, God's volitional will is specific to those He chooses to guide; it is not universal for all.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الإذن
الإباحة
الرخصة
اليسر
العسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that whenever God wills something in the Quran, it means He actively decrees it.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'will' can also mean permission and facilitation, especially in contexts of ease and concession.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi interpret God's will for ease in the verse 'Allah intends for you ease'?
A) As a command to seek ease.
B) As permission, allowance, and facilitation.
C) As a decree that everyone will have ease.
D) As a promise of paradise for all believers.

Key Takeaway
God's will for ease in the Quran is not a decree of faith for all, but a permission and facilitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah intends ease for you, do you truly feel that He permits and facilitates your path, or do you sometimes feel burdened by expectations of perfection in faith? How does understanding His will as permission change how you approach your shortcomings?
?
This lesson distinguishes between God's will as decree and as facilitation. In your own life, where have you mistaken God's permission for His command, or vice versa? Are there areas where you feel guilty for not living up to a standard that is actually just a facilitation, not a requirement?
?
The opponent argued that since disbelief is the greatest hardship, God must will faith for all. But Maturidi says the will in the verse is not about faith. Do you ever assume that your own ease or hardship is a direct sign of God's favor or disfavor? How can this lesson free you from that assumption?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The Conditional Argument: Selective Divine Will
Maturidi argues that if God's will were universally for faith, all would believe, proving His will is selective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument: if God's will were universally for faith, then every disbeliever would believe, which is not the case, proving that God's volitional will is specific to believers.

Core Idea
If God willed faith for all, none would disbelieve; so His will is selective.

Maturidi strengthens his refutation with a logical argument: if God's will for faith were the same as His will for ease (as the opponent claims), then God would will faith for all people. But then all would believe, which is false. This leads to the conclusion that God's volitional will for faith is specific to those He actually guides. The passage supports this with Quranic verses showing God's will for guidance and the Hereafter are selective.

Guided Path
1
Follow the conditional argument
Read the passage carefully to understand Maturidi's logic: if God willed faith for a disbeliever, that disbeliever would believe. Since not all believe, God does not will faith for all.
The argument is based on the absolute efficacy of God's will.
2
See the supporting verses
The passage cites three verses: 6:125 (whoever Allah wants to guide), 3:176 (Allah intends no share in Hereafter for certain people), and 8:67 (Allah desires the Hereafter). Reflect on how they differentiate groups.
3
Connect to the lesson's key point
Understand that this argument establishes that God's will for faith is not universal but particular to believers, contradicting the opponent's inference from ease verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بقوله تعالى: (يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ) [البقرة:185]، ونحو ذلك، وقال: (وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ) [البقرة:185]، والكفر أعسر العسر. قيل: الإرادة في هذا تخرج على الإذن والإباحة والرخصة، ليس ذلك من أمر الإيمان في شيء، فكذا إرادة العسر. وأيضًا أنه لو كان على الأمرين فالوجه [أن] أولئك قوم قد آمنوا فلم يكن لهم في التحقيق غير الذي أراد، فلو كان من الكافر أراد الإيمان لكان لا يكون سواه، كما إذا أراد فيمن أراد الإيمان لم يكن غيره، وعلى هذا قوله تعالى: (فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللَّهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ) [الأنعام:125]، وأيد ذلك قوله: (يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ أَلَّا يَجْعَلَ لَهُمْ حَقًّا فِي الْآخِرَةِ) [آل عمران:176]، وبين في المؤمنين: (وَاللَّهُ يُرِيدُ الْآخِرَةَ) [الأنفال:67]، دل أن كل من أريد له الإيمان أن يكون فعله أراد له الآخرة، ومن لم يرد لا، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he argued with His statement: 'Allah intends for you ease' [2:185], and similar, and He said: 'And does not intend for you hardship' [2:185], and disbelief is the most severe hardship. It was said: The will in this is interpreted as permission, allowance, and concession, that is not from the matter of faith in anything, so likewise the will of hardship. And also if it were upon both matters, the consideration is that those people had believed, so in reality there was nothing other than what He willed. For if He willed for the disbeliever faith, he would be nothing else, just as when He wills for one whom He wills faith, he is nothing else. And upon this is His statement: 'So whoever Allah wants to guide' [6:125], and that is supported by His statement: 'Allah intends that He give them no share in the Hereafter' [3:176], and He made clear concerning the believers: 'And Allah desires the Hereafter' [8:67]. This indicates that for everyone for whom faith is intended, his action is that He intended for him the Hereafter, and for whom He does not intend, not. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 896

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent cites verses where God says He wills ease for people, implying He wills faith (since faith is ease, disbelief is hardship). Maturidi gives the answer: the 'will' here means permission and facilitation (الإذن والإباحة والرخصة), not volitional decree. Disbelief is indeed the greatest hardship, but God does not will it in the sense of permission. Then he adds a second argument: if God's will were only for faith, then believers would have only faith and disbelievers would have faith, but that is not the case. He supports this with verses that show God wills guidance for some but not others, and that He does not will a share in the Hereafter for certain people, while He wills the Hereafter for believers. Thus, God's volitional will is specific to those He chooses to guide; it is not universal for all.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الهداية
الآخرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's second argument, what would happen if God willed faith for a disbeliever?
A) The disbeliever would remain in disbelief as a test.
B) The disbeliever would inevitably believe.
C) The disbeliever would be given a choice to believe.
D) The disbeliever would be punished for not believing.

Key Takeaway
God's will for faith is not universal; it is specific to those He actually guides, as shown by the conditional argument and Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will for your faith is what ultimately determines your belief, do you truly rely on His mercy or do you secretly assume you earn salvation?
?
When you see someone who persists in disbelief, do you pity them and recognize that without God's will even your own faith would vanish, or do you judge them as if you alone chose rightly?
?
The passage says that for those God intends faith, He intends the Hereafter. Are you living today with the certainty that your actions are aligned with His intention for your eternal destiny, or do you live as if the Hereafter is optional?

God's Will for Disbelief is Not Injustice
Maturidi answers the objection from 'Allah does not will injustice'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi reconciles God's will for acts of disbelief with the verse that Allah does not will injustice for His servants.

Core Idea
God's will for an unjust act is not will for injustice toward the servant.

The opponent cites the clear verse that Allah does not will injustice for His servants to argue that He cannot will disbelief, which is considered unjust. Maturidi responds by distinguishing between willing an act that is unjust in itself and willing injustice as a quality of the person. He uses examples from creation and Iblis to show that something can be called false or unjust without its existence being false or unjust.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to the verse about Iblis. Notice how Maturidi immediately counters by saying that if someone wills enmity due to enmity, that is not injustice but justice.
The opponent's proof is the explicit Qur'anic verse. Maturidi does not deny the verse but reinterpret its application.
2
Analyze the Iblis analogy
Maturidi uses the example of Iblis: Allah calls Iblis 'falsehood' but did not create him in vain. Similarly, the act of disbelief is called unjust, but Allah's willing it is not injustice toward the disbeliever.
This shows that an attribute of a thing can be different from the attribute of its existence.
3
Synthesize the key distinction
Understand that Maturidi's key move is to separate the moral quality of the act from the moral quality of the will. God's will for an unjust act is for a wise purpose, not out of tyranny.
This refutes the objection without denying God's power or justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بقول الله تعالى: (وَمَا اللهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ) [غافر:31]. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فنقول كذلك ومن أراد عداوة إنسان له عداوة أو فعله الظلم قبيحا فاحشا، فليس بمريد لهم الظلم بل أراد لهم العدل، قال الله تعالى: (وَمَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاءَ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا بَاطِلًا) [ص:27]، ثم قال في إبليس: (لَا يَأْتِيهِ الْبَاطِلُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَلَا مِنْ خَلْفِهِ) [فصلت:42] سماه باطلا، لا أن خلقه باطلا، فمثله إرادة فعل الكفر من الكافر باطلا وظلما، لا يكون منه إرادة الظلم للعباد، وتأويله قوله: (وَمَا رَبُّكَ بِظَلَّامٍ لِلْعَبِيدِ) [فصلت:46]. وبعد، فإنه في الاعتبار به جائز لأن إرادة ما يعلم أن يكون ليكون عدلا، أمر لم يفعله، والله الكافي أراد جزاء فعله لا أن يعاقبه على [؟]. ثم سئل عن إرادة رسول الله انهزام المشركين فزعم أنه أراد لينظروا فيما دعاهم إليه. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: فالانهزام طاعة أو معصية؟ وكذلك الحال إلى وقت النظر، وفي ذلك دوام على المعصية. لا بد أن يقول: معصية، فيجوز أن يراد به لا على قصدها لبعض المصالح، ومثله قوله: (إِنِّي أُرِيدُ أَنْ تَبُوءَ بِإِثْمِي) [المائدة:29].
And he argued with the statement of Allah Most High: 'And Allah does not will injustice for the servants' [40:31]. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: So we say likewise: Whoever wills enmity for a person, for him is enmity, or his act of injustice is ugly and foul, he is not willing injustice for them but rather he wills justice for them. Allah Most High said: 'And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in vain' [38:27], then He said concerning Iblis: 'Falsehood does not come to it from before it nor from behind it' [41:42]. He called it falsehood, not that its creation is false. So likewise, the will of the act of disbelief from the disbeliever is falsehood and injustice; but it is not from Him will of injustice for the servants, and its interpretation is His statement: 'And your Lord is not unjust to the servants' [41:46]. Moreover, it is permissible in consideration because willing something that He knows will be to be justice, a matter He did not do, and Allah the Sufficient willed the recompense of his action, not that He punishes him for [؟]. Then he was asked about the will of the Messenger of Allah for the defeat of the polytheists, and he claimed that he willed for them to look into what he called them to. The faqih (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Is the defeat an act of obedience or disobedience? And likewise the situation until the time of consideration, and in that there is persistence in disobedience. He must say: disobedience. So it is permissible that it is willed not with the intention of it for some benefit, and similar is His statement: 'Indeed I want you to bear my sin' [5:29].
Source pages p. 897

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent cites the verse that God does not will injustice for His servants, implying He cannot will disbelief (which is unjust). Maturidi answers by distinguishing: if someone wills enmity for another because of that person's enmity, he is not willing injustice but justice. Similarly, God's will for the act of disbelief is not will for injustice as a quality of the servant, but the act itself is unjust. He gives the example of the creation: God did not create in vain, but He calls Iblis false, yet Iblis's creation is not false. So God's will for disbelief is like that: it is an unjust act, but God does not will injustice for the servant; rather, He wills justice. Then he discusses the Prophet's will for the polytheists to be defeated: it is permissible to will a sinful act (their defeat) for a greater benefit (their consideration of faith), similar to the verse 'I want you to bear my sin' where the speaker wills a sinful outcome for a purpose. Thus, God's willing of disbelief is for a wise purpose, not out of injustice.

Key Terms
الظلم
العدل
الباطل
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills an unjust act, then He is unjust and therefore cannot be God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that willing an unjust act for a wise purpose (e.g., as a consequence of the servant's own choice) is not injustice. The injustice lies in the act's moral quality, not in the will itself.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi reconcile God's will for disbelief with the verse 'Allah does not will injustice for the servants'?
A) He says disbelief is not actually injustice.
B) He says God's will for disbelief is not will for the servant's injustice, but for a wise purpose.
C) He says the verse only applies to believers.
D) He denies that God wills disbelief.

Key Takeaway
God's will for an unjust act does not imply injustice toward the servant; the act's moral quality and the will's moral quality are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever resented God's decree in your life, feeling that He was unfair to you? How does knowing that God's will for what appears unjust is actually justice change your perspective on a specific hardship you've faced?
?
If God calls Iblis 'falsehood' and the act of disbelief 'injustice' but still wills it, what does that mean for your own sins? Are you willing to accept full responsibility for your choices, knowing that God's will for them is not unjust?
?
When you sin, do you instinctively blame your circumstances or even God? How can the truth that God wills the act but not the injustice toward you lead you to repent more sincerely today?

Willing a Sin for a Greater Benefit
Maturidi discusses the permissibility of willing a sin for a benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and scriptural basis for God or His prophets willing a sinful act for a greater good, such as guiding people to faith.

Core Idea
A sin can be willed for a benefit, not for itself.

The objection extends to the Prophet's will that the polytheists be defeated, which is a sin (disobedience). Maturidi and the faqih discuss whether such a will is permissible. They conclude that it is permissible to will a sin for a greater benefit, as shown by the verse 'I want you to bear my sin' where a sinful outcome is willed for a purpose. This supports the idea that God may will disbelief for a wise purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and discussion
Read from 'Then he was asked about the will of the Messenger...' to the end. Note that the question is whether the Prophet willed the defeat of the polytheists, which is a sinful act.
The faqih clarifies that defeat is a sin (disobedience) and that it is permissible to will it for a benefit.
2
Analyze the faqih's reasoning
The faqih challenges: is defeat obedience or disobedience? It must be disobedience, but it can be willed not for itself but for the benefit of leading them to consider faith.
This shows that the intention behind the will matters.
3
Connect to the main argument
Maturidi uses this to analogize: God wills disbelief not for its own sake but for the greater good of testing, justice, or divine wisdom.
This refutes the claim that God cannot will sin because it is unjust.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

God's Will is Only for Justice, Not Oppression
Maturidi refutes the claim that God wills injustice for His servants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that God intends injustice for His servants, and how he distinguishes between the intention behind actions and the nature of the actions themselves.

Core Idea
God creates all actions but only intends justice; evil acts are unjust from the creature's side, not from God's intention.

Maturidi addresses a critical objection: if God creates disbelief and evil, does He not intend injustice? In this passage, he clarifies that God's will is always for justice, and He never intends oppression. The key is distinguishing the intention from the act itself. Let's read his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from the objection to Maturidi's response.
2
Identify the objection being refuted
The objection is that if God creates disbelief and evil, He must intend injustice. Maturidi counters using the verse 'And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants.'
This verse is the starting point of the argument.
3
Understand the analogy of enmity
Maturidi gives an analogy: a person who intends enmity toward an enemy acts with ugly injustice, but he does not intend injustice per se; rather, he intends a response. Similarly, God's will for the act of disbelief is not an intention of injustice.
The analogy helps separate intention from the ethical quality of the act.
4
Note the scriptural references
Maturidi cites verses about creation not being in vain (38:27), and about falsehood not coming to the Qur'an (41:42). He explains that Iblis is called falsehood but not created falsehood. This shows that the created act can be evil from the creature's side.
These verses reinforce that God's creation has purpose and is not vain.
5
Conclude with the distinction
The passage concludes that God does not intend injustice for the servants, citing 'And your Lord is not unjust to the servants' (41:46). The key takeaway is that the intention is always justice, even when the created act is unjust on the servant's part.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بقول الله تعالى : وَمَا اللهُ يُرِدُ ظُلْمًا لِلعِبَادِ . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فنقول كذلك ومن أراد عداوة إنسان له عداوة أو فعله الظلم قبيحا فاحشا ، فليس بمريد لهم الظلم بل أراد لهم العدل ، قال الله تعالى : وَمَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاءَ وَالأَرضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا بَاطِلا . ثم قال في إبليس : لا يَأْتِيهِ البَاطِلُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَلَا مِنْ خَلْفِهِ سماه باطلا ، لا أن خلقه باطلا ، فمثله إرادة فعل الكفر من الكافر باطلا وظلما ، لا يكون منه إرادة الظلم للعباد ، وتأويله قوله : وَمَا رَبُّكَ بِظَلَّامٍ لِلْعَبِيدِ .
And he argued by the saying of Allah Most High: 'And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants.' The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: So we say likewise: And whoever intends enmity of a person who has enmity for him, or his action of injustice is ugly and foul, then he is not intending injustice for them, but rather he intends justice for them. Allah Most High said: 'And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in vain.' Then He said about Iblis: 'Falsehood does not come to it from before it or from behind it' — He called it falsehood, not that He created it falsehood. So likewise, the intention of the act of disbelief from the disbeliever is falsehood and injustice; there is not from Him an intention of injustice for the servants. And its interpretation is His saying: 'And your Lord is not unjust to the servants.'
Source pages p. 897

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection based on the verse 'And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants' (Qur'an 40:31). The objector argues that if God creates disbelief and evil, He must be intending injustice. Maturidi responds by distinguishing between the intention behind an action and the action itself. He gives the analogy of a person who intends enmity toward an enemy—his action of harming is ugly, but his intention is not injustice per se but to respond to enmity. He then cites Qur'an 38:27, where God says He did not create the heavens and the earth in vain, implying that creation has purpose. Then he refers to Iblis (Satan) in Qur'an 41:42, where falsehood is said not to come to the Qur'an, but Iblis is called falsehood—yet God did not create Iblis falsehood; rather, Iblis's actions are false. Similarly, God's will for the disbeliever's act of disbelief is not an intention of injustice but a creation of an act that is inherently unjust and false from the servant's side. The passage ends by citing Qur'an 41:46: 'And your Lord is not unjust to the servants' to confirm that God does not intend oppression.

Key Terms
إرادة (will/intention)
ظلم (injustice/oppression)
باطل (falsehood)
خلق (creation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because God creates evil acts, He intends evil.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the act is evil from the creature's side, not from God's intention. God's will is always for justice and wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, why does God's creation of disbelief not constitute intending injustice?
A) Because God's will is separate from His actions.
B) Because the act of disbelief is falsehood from the servant's side, not from God's intention.
C) Because God only wills justice, and injustice is impossible for Him.
D) Because God created disbelief as a test, not as injustice.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that God's will is not tainted by the evil of the acts He creates because He does not intend injustice; rather, the acts themselves are unjust from the servant's perspective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or witness suffering, do you ever feel that God has dealt unjustly with you? Reflect on the fact that God never intends injustice. How might this truth change your response to hardship? Will you actively fight that feeling of injustice and trust in His perfect justice?
?
Think of a time you committed a sin. Did you blame your circumstances or even God for your actions? Today's lesson teaches that the injustice of sin is from you, not from God's will. Will you take full responsibility for your sins and turn to Him in repentance, knowing He only intends good for you?
?
You call yourself a believer, yet how often do you assume God wrongs you when things don't go your way? This is a subtle form of disbelief in His names al-‘Adl (the Just) and al-Hakam (the Judge). Will you repent of this, and from now on, when you face disappointment, say 'My Lord does not intend injustice for me'? Make a firm resolve to do so today.

Willing a Sinful Act for a Good Purpose
Maturidi addresses whether the Prophet's will for defeat of polytheists was a sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi distinction between intending an act that is sinful and intending sin itself, and how this applies to divine will and prophetic actions.

Core Idea
Willing an act that results in sin is not the same as willing sin.

Maturidi addresses a theological objection: Did the Prophet sin by willing the defeat of the polytheists? He clarifies that one can intend an act for a good purpose even if the act is sinful from a certain perspective. The key is the intention behind the will, not the outcome alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the jurist's question
Read the opening sentence to understand the objection: Is defeat (inhiyam) obedience or disobedience? The jurist presents a dilemma.
2
Follow the response and examples
Note how Maturidi (through the jurist's voice) replies that the act is disobedience but can be willed not for its sinfulness but for a benefit. Examine the Qur'anic example (5:29) and the analogy with believers' sins.
The distinction hinges on the intention (qasd) behind the will.
3
Apply the principle to divine will
Consider how Maturidi extends the argument to God's will: Allah wills the disbeliever's act of disbelief for some wisdom, not for the sin itself. This preserves divine justice.
This connects to the broader theological principle that God's will for an act does not imply He wills the sinfulness in it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : فالانهزام طاعة أو معصية ؟ وكذلك الحال إلى وقت النظر ، وفى ذلك دوام على المعصية . لا بد أن يقول : معصية ، فيجوز أن يراد به لا على قصدها لبعض المصالح ، ومثله قوله : إِنِّي أُرِيدُ أَنْ تَبُوا بِإِثْمِي . إنه يجوز إرادة فعل هو معصية لا على قصد عصيان ، وكذلك وكذلك معاصي المؤمنين كلها كانت على أفعال من عاصيهم ، وإن لم يريدوا معصية الله ، بل لو أرادوا لكفروا ، فهذا يبين أن إرادة فعل يكون من فاعله معصية لا يكون كإرادة المعصية ، فمثله إرادة الله فعل الكافر ليكون منه معصية ، أو فعله الشتم ليكون مشتما قبيحا لا يكون كإرادة الشتم والمعصية ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The jurist (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Is defeat (inhiyam) obedience or disobedience? And similarly the situation until the time of consideration, and in that is persistence in disobedience. It must be said: Disobedience. Then it is permissible that it be intended not with the intention of it for some benefit. An example of that is His saying: 'I intend that you should bear my sin.' Indeed, it is permissible to intend an act that is disobedience not with the intention of disobedience. And likewise all the acts of disobedience of the believers were acts from those who disobeyed them, even though they did not intend disobedience to Allah; rather, if they had intended it, they would have disbelieved. This clarifies that the intention of an act that is disobedience from its doer is not like the intention of disobedience. Similarly, Allah's intention of the disbeliever's act to be disobedience from him, or His act of reviling to be reviling and ugly, is not like the intention of reviling and disobedience. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 897

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from a jurist (al-faqih) concerning the Prophet's desire for the defeat (inhiyam) of the polytheists. The jurist asks whether that defeat is obedience or disobedience. He argues that if it is disobedience, then it implies the Prophet intended a sinful outcome. Maturidi (via the jurist's voice?) clarifies that an act can be willed not for its sinful nature but for some other benefit. He cites the Qur'anic verse (5:29) where a person says 'I intend that you should bear my sin,' meaning the intention is for the other to bear it, not to commit sin per se. He then applies this to all sins of believers: they perform acts that are sinful but do not intend disobedience to God; if they intended disobedience, they would be disbelievers. Thus, the intention of an act that happens to be sinful is not the same as intending sin. Analogously, God's will for the disbeliever's act of disbelief is not an intention of sin; rather, He intends the act for some wisdom, while the sinfulness comes from the doer's side. The passage emphasizes that God's will for an act that is sinful is not like a creature's will for sin.

Key Terms
إرادة (will/intention)
معصية (disobedience/sin)
طاعة (obedience)
قصد (intention/purpose)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God wills a sinful act, He intends sin.
Correction: This passage clarifies that God's will is for the act's existence, not for its sinfulness. God intends the act for a wise purpose, while the sinfulness is attributed to the doer's free will.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction Maturidi makes in this passage regarding divine will?
A) God's will is only for obedience, not for sinful acts.
B) Intending an act that results in sin is different from intending sin itself.
C) Sinful acts are never willed by God.
D) The Prophet's will for defeat was actually obedience.

Key Takeaway
The intention of an act that results in sin is distinct from intending sin itself, which preserves divine justice and prophetic impeccability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you committed a sin but told yourself it was for a 'good reason.' What was your actual intention? Were you truly aiming for the benefit, or were you deceiving yourself? This lesson demands you examine the state of your heart. Will you confess that to Allah and repent with sincerity?
?
Do you ever blame Allah for your sins, thinking He willed them so you are not responsible? This lesson clarifies that God's will for the act is not His intention for the sin. How will you take full ownership of your intentions from now on? What will you do the next time you feel tempted to shift blame?
?
If you believe that God is just, why do you still sin? Because you intend the act for some worldly gain, not the sin itself? Or because you simply ignore His commands? Let this lesson pierce your heart: your intention matters to God. Will you commit to checking your intentions before every action today?

Refuting the Claim of the Prophet's Pleasure with the Polytheists' Defeat
Maturidi refutes the claim that the Prophet's pleasure implies divine approval of the polytheists' defeat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet's pleasure regarding the polytheists' defeat was not for the sake of Allah or the Prophet himself, thus the objection is invalid.

Core Idea
Pleasure in divine context depends on the purpose of the action, not mere outcome.

When an objector claimed that the Prophet was pleased with the defeat of the polytheists, implying that the defeat was divinely approved, Maturidi quickly counters by analyzing the object of that pleasure. He argues that the defeat was not for the Prophet or for God, so it cannot be framed in terms of divine pleasure or displeasure. This lesson unpacks that crisp rejoinder.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the objection and Maturidi's response.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims the Prophet was pleased (رضى) with the defeat. Why would that be problematic?
If the Prophet was pleased, doesn't that imply divine approval?
3
Analyze Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi says the defeat was not for the Prophet or Allah, so one cannot speak of pleasure/displeasure in that context.
He distinguishes between actions done for Allah and those that are not.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
This shows that divine pleasure is tied to the purpose and agent of the action, not just the outcome.
Consider how this relates to Maturidi's overall theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض بأن رسول الله رضى منهم . ، وذلك فاسد ؛ لأن الانهزام منهم لم يكن لرسول الله أو لله ، فيتكلم فيه بالرضا وغير الرضا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم عورض بما كان كفر أكثر عباد الله بأن أراد إبليس ، والله أراد منهم الطاعة ، فصارت إرادة إبليس فى ملك الله وسلطانه أبعد من إرادة الله ، فأجاب بالرضا والمحبة والسخط ، وقد بينا نحن الفصل بين الأمرين ، على أنه يكون فعل يرضى به المرء ويسخط من غير أن كان وقت فعله ، ومحال ذلك في الإرادة ثبت أنها شرط الفعل فيما يظهر التعجيز إذ لا يخلو عنها فعل المختار . وأيضا إنا لا نقول بأن الله يحب من يعلم أنه لا يؤمن أو يرضى منه لأنهما يحبان بالفعل ، فمن لا يفعل بالقول به بعيد ، وأما الإرادة فقد بينا ، والله أعلم .
Then he objected that the Messenger of Allah was pleased with them. And that is invalid because the defeat from them was not for the Messenger of Allah or for Allah, so one speaks about it with pleasure and displeasure. And there is no power except with Allah. Then he was objected to with the fact that the disbelief of most of Allah's servants was because Iblis willed, and Allah willed obedience from them, so the will of Iblis in the kingdom and authority of Allah is more distant from Allah's will. He answered by (the concepts of) pleasure, love, and anger. And we have already explained the distinction between the two matters: that there is an action that one is pleased with and angry at without it being at the time of its occurrence. That is impossible for will, it being established that will is a condition of action in what appears to be incapacity, since the action of the free agent cannot be free of it. Also, we do not say that Allah loves those whom He knows will not believe or is pleased with them, because these (love and pleasure) are with action. So whoever does not act is far from saying that. As for will, we have already explained. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 898

Commentary
This passage addresses two objections. First, an objector claims that the Prophet was pleased with the defeat of the polytheists, implying that the act was good. Maturidi refutes this by noting that the defeat was not for the Prophet or for God, so it cannot be said to be pleasing or displeasing in the same sense. Second, an objector argues that since Iblis willed disbelief for most people and God willed obedience from them, Iblis's will seems to prevail over God's will in His own kingdom. Maturidi answers by distinguishing between pleasure/love/anger and will. He explains that pleasure and anger can relate to an action after its occurrence without having caused it, but will is a condition for the action—a free agent's action cannot occur without will. Thus, God's will is efficient, whereas Iblis's will is not. Additionally, Maturidi clarifies that God does not love or feel pleasure toward those whom He knows will not believe, because love and pleasure are based on actual action, not mere preordination. The passage concludes by reaffirming the previous explanation about will.

Key Terms
رضا (pleasure/satisfaction)
إرادة (will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's personal emotions always indicate divine approval.
Correction: Maturidi shows that pleasure is context-dependent; not every event that pleases a human is divinely approved.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the objection about the Prophet's pleasure invalid?
A) Because the Prophet never experienced pleasure at that event.
B) Because the defeat was not for the Prophet or Allah, so pleasure/displeasure cannot be attributed to it.
C) Because the Prophet's pleasure is irrelevant to divine will.
D) Because the polytheists' defeat was actually displeasing to the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's pleasure at an event does not automatically signify divine approval; the action's purpose and relation to Allah must be considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel pleased about an outcome in your life, do you ever assume that God must be pleased too? How does this lesson challenge you to separate your emotions from divine approval?
?
Can you recall a situation where you were happy about something that later harmed your faith? What does that teach you about the need to evaluate events by their purpose and relation to Allah, not by your feelings?
?
The Prophet's pleasure at the polytheists' defeat could have been misunderstood as divine endorsement. What are some modern examples where people confuse human reactions with God's will? How can you guard your own heart against this error?

Distinguishing Divine Will from Pleasure in Responding to Iblis's Will Objection
Maturidi distinguishes will from pleasure/love to refute the objection about Iblis's will prevailing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's key distinction between will (which necessitates action) and pleasure/anger (which can be after action), and how this resolves the objection that Iblis's will appears stronger than God's.

Core Idea
Will is a condition for action; pleasure can come after without causing it.

A more serious objection arises: if Iblis wills disbelief for most people, and God wills obedience from them, then Iblis's will seems to prevail in God's own kingdom. Maturidi responds by drawing a sharp distinction between will, which is a necessary condition for the occurrence of an action, and attributes like pleasure and love, which can relate to an action after its occurrence. This lesson delves into that crucial theological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the objection and Maturidi's answer.
The passage is longer and involves multiple concepts.
2
Identify the objection
The objector says: Iblis willed disbelief, God willed obedience, but disbelief happened. So Iblis's will seems more effective.
This challenges God's sovereignty.
3
Understand Maturidi's distinction
He introduces pleasure (رضا), love (محبة), and anger (سخط) as distinct from will. Pleasure can be after an action, but will is a condition of the action. So Iblis's will is not causal; only God's will is.
The key point: will must be present for an action to occur, but pleasure can be retrospective.
4
Apply the distinction
Thus, Iblis's will to disbelieve does not cause disbelief; rather, God's will permits it or decrees it. But God does not love or take pleasure in disbelief.
Maturidi adds that love and pleasure are based on actual action, not preordination.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
This preserves God's sovereignty and justice: He wills all that occurs, but He is not pleased with evil actions.
Consider how this relates to Maturidi's broader theology of free will and divine attributes.

Maturidi refutes this by noting that the defeat was not for the Prophet or for God, so it cannot be said to be pleasing or displeasing in the same sense. Divine Will: The Only Source of Human Action
Maturidi establishes that God's will is necessary for every voluntary human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that every human action must be by God's will, and how he refutes the Mu'tazila's denial of a distinct attribute of will.

Core Idea
Every action stems from will, compulsion, or forgetfulness—and only will fits God.

Maturidi begins with a simple observation about human actions: they always fall into one of three categories. He then applies this to God, showing that compulsion and forgetfulness are impossible for Him, so His will must be the source. He also addresses the Mu'tazila, who reduce will to mere knowledge, arguing that their position is inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three categories of action: will, compulsion, forgetfulness.
2
Understand the logical deduction
See why Maturidi eliminates compulsion and forgetfulness for God, leaving will as the only possibility.
Apply the principle that God cannot be described with imperfection.
3
Analyze the critique of Mu'tazila
Understand their view (will as mere knowledge) and Maturidi's counter-argument that this meaning exists in all actions.
Consider why their denial is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل فى هذا فى المتعارف أن الفعل يخرج على إرادة أو غلبة أو غفلة ، الله سبحانه لا يجوز أن يوصف فى فعل العبد بالغلبة أو بالغفلة ، ثبت أن كان بالإرادة . والمعتزلة لا يثبتون لله معنى في الإرادة سوى كون العلم بعد يكن من غير ضرورة له ، وهذا المعنى هو فى فعل كل من أهل العالم قائم ، فلا وجه لإنكارهم على قولهم ، وبالله العصمة .
And the principle in this matter, according to common usage, is that action proceeds from either will, compulsion, or forgetfulness. Allah, exalted be He, cannot be described in the action of the servant with compulsion or forgetfulness. So it is established that it is by will. And the Mu'tazila do not affirm for Allah a meaning (ma'na) in will other than knowledge that it will be without necessity for Him. And this meaning exists in the action of everyone among the people. So there is no basis for their denial of their statement. And with Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 898

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational principle: in common understanding, every action occurs either by will (irada), compulsion (ghalba), or forgetfulness (ghafla). Since it is impossible to attribute compulsion or forgetfulness to Allah in relation to the servant's action, it must be that the action occurs by Allah's will. This serves as a rational proof for the necessity of divine will. Maturidi then critiques the Mu'tazila, who deny that God has a distinct attribute of will. They consider will to be merely knowledge of the outcome without any need (gharar). Maturidi argues that this same meaning (of knowledge) exists in all people's actions, so their denial is inconsistent. The passage ends with a prayer for divine protection.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
غلبة (compulsion/force)
غفلة (forgetfulness)
معنى (meaning/attribute)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Actions can occur without God's will.
Correction: Maturidi argues that every action must be by will, compulsion, or forgetfulness; since compulsion and forgetfulness are impossible for God, His will is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why must human actions be by God's will?
A) Because God is the only agent.
B) Because actions cannot proceed from compulsion or forgetfulness in God's case.
C) Because the Mu'tazila deny will.
D) Because the Qur'an says so.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates through a simple classification that God's will is the necessary source of every human action, because compulsion and forgetfulness are impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a recent action of yours, perhaps a mistake or a moment of obedience. Do you truly believe that God's will was its sole source, or do you feel that your own will operated independently? How does the answer affect your sense of reliance on Him?
?
When you sin, do you ever think, 'God didn't will this for me'? If so, how does this passage challenge that belief? If you accept that God willed it, how do you reconcile that with your responsibility? What would change in your repentance if you fully internalized this truth?
?
The Mu'tazila made God's will a mere knowledge, perhaps to protect human responsibility. But does that protect God's perfection? Where in your life have you reduced God's attributes to fit your understanding, rather than submitting to His revelation? What step can you take today to affirm His will over your own?

Proof That God’s Will Is the Agent of Human Acts
Maturidi gives a proof and refutation on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that human actions are caused by God's will through the elimination of force and heedlessness, and his refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that God's will is merely knowledge.

Core Idea
God's will is the only possible cause of human actions because compulsion and heedlessness are impossible for Him.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise proof that every act must arise from will, force, or heedlessness. Since God cannot be subject to force or heedlessness, human actions must be attributed to His will. He then addresses the Mu'tazilite objection that God's will is simply His knowledge, showing this view fails to distinguish divine will from human will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains two distinct moves: a proof and a refutation.
2
Identify the tripartite classification
The passage states that acts proceed from will, force, or heedlessness. Understand each term and why this classification is considered exhaustive for voluntary acts.
This is an empirical observation from common parlance.
3
Apply the classification to God
Since force and heedlessness are impossible for God, the only remaining cause is will. This is the core of the proof.
Ask yourself why force and heedlessness are impossible for God.
4
Examine the Mu'tazilite refutation
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for reducing God's will to mere knowledge. See why he finds this inadequate.
Consider how this argument defends the reality of God's attribute of will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في هذا في المتعارف أن الفعل يخرج على إرادة أو غلبة أو غفلة، الله سبحانه لا يجوز أن يوصف في فعل العبد بالغلبة أو بالغفلة، ثبت أن كان بالإرادة. والمعتزلة لا يثبتون لله معنى في الإرادة سوى كون العلم بعد يكن من غير ضرورة له، وهذا المعنى هو في فعل كل من أهل العالم قائم، ثم فلا وجه لإنكارهم على قولهم، وبالله العصمة.
The principle regarding this in common parlance is that the act proceeds from will, force, or heedlessness. God, exalted, cannot be described with respect to the servant's act as having force or heedlessness, so it is established that it is by will. The Mu'tazila do not affirm for God a meaning in will beyond knowledge that it is [that the act will occur] without necessity for Him. But this meaning is present in the action of every inhabitant of the world. Then there is no basis for their denial of [this] statement. And with God is preservation.
Source pages p. 898

Commentary
This passage contains a rational proof and a refutation of the Mu'tazila. Maturidi begins by stating an empirical classification: in common experience, every voluntary act is caused either by will (irada), by compulsion (ghalaba), or by heedlessness (ghafla). Since God is absolutely free from compulsion and heedlessness, the only remaining possibility for His causing human acts is will. He then attacks the Mu'tazila position that God's will is merely knowledge of the act. Maturidi argues that such a notion of will is found in every human being (as knowledge of the act), so it fails to distinguish God's will. Hence, the Mu'tazila denial of a real attribute of will is unfounded.

Key Terms
إرادة
غلبة
غفلة
علم
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila belief that God's will is merely His knowledge of the act.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such a notion of will is present in every human, so it does not capture the distinctive nature of God's will.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why must human actions be attributed to God's will rather than force or heedlessness?
A) Because God is all-powerful.
B) Because force and heedlessness are impossible for God.
C) Because God knows everything.
D) Because human actions are free.

Key Takeaway
Human actions are caused by God's will because force and heedlessness cannot be attributed to God, and God's will is a real attribute distinct from mere knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action you do is ultimately caused by God's will, does that make you feel powerless or comforted? Why? How does this truth change the way you view your own successes and failures?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed God's will is just knowledge—are there areas of your life where you treat God's will as mere information rather than as the active cause of everything that happens? What would change if you truly believed His will is the real agent?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel God has left you? According to this passage, God's will is always the agent—so in your moments of disobedience, what does that say about His relationship to your sin? How does that challenge your assumptions about God's involvement in your life?

Divine Will vs. Wishful Thinking
Maturidi distinguishes divine will from wishful thinking and refutes coercion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will is effective while Satan's wish is impotent, and why faith cannot be coerced.

Core Idea
Wishful thinking cannot overcome divine will; coercion invalidates faith.

Maturidi begins by addressing a key misunderstanding about the divine attribute of will. He contrasts the effective will of Allah with the mere wish of Iblis, showing that wishful thinking lacks the power to overcome divine determination. This distinction leads to a refutation of the idea that God could or should force belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the contrast between 'irāda' and 'tamannī'.
2
Identify the two wills
The passage mentions the will of Iblis (wishful thinking) and the will of Allah (effective). See how Maturidi uses a reported statement to set up the distinction.
3
Understand the objection and reply
Al-Faqih objects that if God can prevent disbelief, He should. The reply explains that will sometimes overcomes, but wish does not. Then it condemns faith by coercion as an effect of confusion.
This shows that divine power does not imply coercion.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why coercion is impermissible—faith must be chosen, not forced. The passage guards against the sectarian view that God compels disbelief.
This is a core Maturidi position on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : إرادة إبليس هي التمني ، ولو أراد العباد ما كفروا ، والله يقدر على منعهم بالقهر قال الفقيه رحمه الله : قلنا له قد صدقت، والإرادة قد توجب الغلبة والتمني لا ، فكيف غلب تمني عدوه على إرادته ؟ وقوله : يقدر ويقهر ، وهذا النوع إنما هو أثر الحيرة والوحشة ، ولا يجوز الإيمان بالقسر بوجه . ثم قال : فإن قيل : رأيت حكيما يقدر على منع عبده عن أمر لا يريده ولا يمنحه ؟ فعارض بالجبر ، وذا خطأ ؛ لأنه عندنا يريده ، وليس المنع من شرط ما يراد . ثم فإنه يعد لوجهين لا يريده ولا يجوز له المنع لضرب من التدبير قال الشيخ رحمه الله : إن كنت على الشاهد تقدره فلا تجاده ، إلا أن لا يقدر عليه أو هو لم يرد الفعل به . قال : ومنها ما يجب المنع ، فدل أن المنع إن وجب وجب لعلة لا لعينه ، وما يذكر من العلة فإن كانت أوجبت الاضطرار ، فهو الذي قيل لا يقدر عليه ، وإن كان لا يوجب ، وقد يملك القهر لا بالتعدى ، فهو لا يسعه عندنا ، وهو خارج من العرف ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he said: The will of Iblis is wishful thinking. And if the servants had willed, they would not have disbelieved. And God is able to prevent them by force. Al-Faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: We said to him: You have spoken the truth. Will sometimes necessitates overcoming, but wishful thinking does not. So how did the wishful thinking of His enemy overcome His will? And His saying: "He is able and compels" – this type is only the effect of confusion and alienation, and faith through coercion is not permissible in any way. Then he said: If it is said: Do you see a wise person who is able to prevent his servant from something he does not will and does not grant him? He objected by coercion (jabr), and that is an error; because according to us, He wills it, and prevention is not a condition of what is willed. Then it is counted for two aspects: He does not will it, and it is not permissible for Him to prevent it for a kind of management. The Sheikh (may God have mercy on him) said: If you base it on the witnessed, you would not find it, unless He is not able to do it or He did not will the action by it. He said: Among them is what necessitates prevention. So it indicates that prevention, if it is necessary, is necessary for a cause, not for itself. And the cause mentioned, if it necessitates compulsion, then it is that which it was said He is not able to do. And if it does not necessitate, and He may possess force without transgression, then it is not within His capacity according to us, and it is outside of custom. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 899

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument defending divine predestination against the charge of coercion (jabr). It begins by distinguishing between divine will (إرادة), which is effective, and Satan's wish (تمني), which is impotent. The interlocutor objects that if God has power to prevent disbelief by force, He should do so. The answer states that faith by force is impermissible and would be an effect of confusion (حيرة) and alienation (وحشة). Another objection is raised: a wise master would prevent his servant from doing what he does not will; why doesn't God do likewise? The reply clarifies that God does will the servant's act, and prevention is not a necessary condition of what is willed. The sheikh then explains that prevention, when it occurs, is for a reason (علة), not arbitrarily. If the reason necessitates compulsion, that cannot be attributed to God; if it does not, then forcible prevention is outside His custom and not permissible. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that divine power implies arbitrary coercion in matters of faith.

Key Terms
إرادة
تمني
جبر
قسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine will is similar to human wishful thinking, or that God's power to prevent disbelief implies He should force faith.
Correction: Divine will is effective and always prevails, while wishful thinking is impotent. Faith by coercion is an effect of confusion and is impermissible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is faith by coercion not permissible?
A) Because God does not have power over it.
B) Because will sometimes necessitates overcoming, but wishful thinking does not.
C) Because it is an effect of confusion and alienation.
D) Because it is outside of custom.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is effective and cannot be overcome by mere wishes; coerced faith is impermissible because it stems from confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hope for something without effort—like faith in your heart without action—are you engaging in wishful thinking? How will you turn that hope into genuine submission to God's will today?
?
Have you ever wished that God would force someone you love to believe? What does this lesson's rejection of coerced faith teach you about the dignity of free choice?
?
In what area of your life do you secretly hold onto your own wishes while claiming to submit to God's will? What would it look like to truly align your desires with His effective will as described in this passage?

The Nature of Prevention and Coercion Refuted
Maturidi answers the objection about prevention and clarifies the role of cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God does not prevent all sin by force, and how prevention, when it occurs, is for a reason (ʿilla).

Core Idea
Prevention of sin is not a condition of divine will; it occurs only for a wise cause.

Maturidi continues by addressing a further objection: if God is wise and able, why does He not prevent His servant from doing what He does not will? The response clarifies that God does will the action (in terms of creation), and prevention is not a necessary condition. Moreover, prevention itself, when it happens, is always for a reason (ʿilla), not arbitrarily.

Guided Path
1
Read the next passage
This passage continues the argument. Read the Arabic and translation together.
It builds on the previous lesson.
2
Identify the objection
An objector says: a wise master prevents his servant from doing what he does not will. Why doesn't God do that?
3
Follow the reply
Maturidi refutes the objection: God does will the act (in a certain sense), and prevention is not a condition of will. He then adds that prevention is either due to a cause (ʿilla) or not; if due to a cause, it may necessitate compulsion, which cannot be attributed to God.
The sheikh adds that if you look at the witnessed world, prevention only occurs when the agent cannot do the act or does not will it.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The final sentence states that prevention without cause is outside God's custom and not permissible. Hence, God does not arbitrarily coerce.
This reinforces the Maturidi rejection of jabr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the passage, why does God not prevent His servant from sinning?

?

?

?
This lesson says prevention only occurs with a cause, and arbitrary coercion is outside God's custom. Look at your own life: is there a particular failure you have resented God for not stopping? Write down one such moment, then ask yourself: what wise purpose might that 'unprevented' act have served in your soul? Are you willing to let that purpose transform you instead of blaming God?
